until\\n241:      * authentication succeeds or fails.\\n242:      *\\n243:      * The messages are sent and received as size delimited bytes that consists of a 4 byte network-ordered size N\\n244:      * followed by N bytes representing the opaque payload.\\n245:      */\\n246:     @SuppressWarnings(\\\"fallthrough\\\")\\n247:     @Override\\n248:     public void authenticate() throws IOException {\\n249:         if (saslState != SaslState.REAUTH_PROCESS_HANDSHAKE) {\\n250:             if (netOutBuffer != null && !flushNetOutBufferAndUpdateInterestOps())\\n251:                 return;\\n252: \\n253:             if (saslServer != null && saslServer.isComplete()) {\\n254:                 setSaslState(SaslState.COMPLETE);\\n255:                 return;\\n256:             }\\n257: \\n258: connectionId);\\n260: \\n261:             try {\\n262:                 netInBuffer.readFrom(transportLayer);\\n263:             } catch (InvalidReceiveException e) {\\n264:                 throw new      break;\\n285:                     // For default GSSAPI, fall through to authenticate using the client token as the first GSSAPI packet.\\n286:                     // This is required for interoperability with 0.9.0.x clients which do not send handshake request\\n287:                 case AUTHENTICATE:\\n288:                     handleSaslToken(clientToken);\\n289:                     // When the authentication exchange is complete and no more tokens are expected from the client,\\n290:                     // update SASL state. }\\n335: \\n336:     @Override\\n337:     public void close() throws IOException {\\n338:         if (principalBuilder instanceof Closeable)\\n339:             Utils.closeQuietly((Closeable) KafkaPrincipal.ANONYMOUS, listenerName, securityProtocol, ClientInformation.EMPTY, false);\\n442:             RequestAndSize requestAndSize = requestContext.parseRequest(requestBuffer);\\n443: // This should not normally occur since clients typically check supported versions using ApiVersionsRequest\\n451:                 throw new UnsupportedVersionException(\\\"Version \\\" + version + \\n504:     private boolean handleKafkaRequest(byte[] requestBytes) throws IOException, AuthenticationException {\\n505:         boolean isKafkaRequest = false;\\n506:         String clientMechanism = null;\\n507:         try {\\n508:             ByteBuffer requestBuffer = ByteBuffer.wrap(requestBytes);\\n509:             RequestHeader header = RequestHeader.parse(requestBuffer);\\n510:             ApiKeys apiKey = header.apiKey();\\n511: \\n512:             // A valid Kafka request header was received. This avoids\\n519:             // unnecessary exposure to some of the more complex schema types.\\n520:             if (apiKey != ApiKeys.API_VERSIONS && apiKey != ApiKeys.SASL_HANDSHAKE)\\n521:                 throw new IllegalSaslStateException(\\\"Unexpected Kafka request of type \\\" + apiKey + \\\" during SASL handshake.\\\");\\n522: \\n523:             LOG.debug(\\\"Handling Kafka request {} during {}\\\", apiKey, reauthInfo.authenticationOrReauthenticationText());\\n524: \\n525: \\n526:             RequestContext requestContext = new RequestContext(header, connectionId, clientAddress(), Optional.of(clientPort()),\\n527:                     KafkaPrincipal.ANONYMOUS, listenerName, securityProtocol, ClientInformation.EMPTY, false);\\n528:             RequestAndSize requestAndSize = requestContext.parseRequest(requestBuffer);\\n529:             if (apiKey == ApiKeys.API_VERSIONS)\\n530:                 handleApiVersionsRequest(requestContext, (ApiVersionsRequest) requestAndSize.request);\\n531:             else\\n532:                 clientMechanism = handleHandshakeRequest(requestContext, (SaslHandshakeRequest) requestAndSize.request);\\n533:         } catch (InvalidRequestException e) {\\n534:             if (saslState == SaslState.INITIAL_REQUEST) {\\n535:                 // InvalidRequestException is thrown if the request is not in Kafka format or if the API key\\n536:                 // is invalid. (LOG.isDebugEnabled()) {\\n539:                     StringBuilder tokenBuilder = new StringBuilder();\\n540:                     for (byte b : requestBytes) {\\n541:                         tokenBuilder.append(String.format(\\\"%02x\\\", b));\\n542:                         if (tokenBuilder.length() >= 20)\\n543:                              break;\\n544:                     }\\n545:                     LOG.debug(\\\"Received client packet of length {} starting with bytes 0x{}, process as GSSAPI packet\\\", requestBytes.length, tokenBuilder);\\n546:                 }\\n547: UnsupportedSaslMechanismException(\\\"Exception handling first SASL packet from client, GSSAPI is not supported by server\\\", e);\\n552:             } else\\n553:                 throw e;\\n554: handleHandshakeRequest(RequestContext context, SaslHandshakeRequest handshakeRequest) throws IOException, UnsupportedSaslMechanismException {\\n564:         String clientMechanism = handshakeRequest.data().mechanism();\\n565:         short version = context.header.apiVersion();\\n566:         if (version >= 1)\\n567:             this.enableKafkaSaslAuthenticateHeaders(true);\\n568:         if (enabledMechanisms.contains(clientMechanism)) {\\n569:             LOG.debug(\\\"Using SASL mechanism '{}' provided by client\\\", I'll look at each file:\n\n## 1. ScramSaslServer.java\n\nLooking at the authentication logic, specifically the `evaluateResponse` method:\n\n**Line 132**: Authorization ID check\n```java\nif (!authorizationIdFromClient.isEmpty() && !authorizationIdFromClient.equals(username))\n    throw new SaslAuthenticationException(...)\n```\nThis looks correct - it checks that the authorization ID from the client matches the username.\n\n**Line 135-136**: Iteration count check\n```java\nif (scramCredential.iterations() < mechanism.minIterations())\n    throw new SaslException(...)\n```\nThis is good - it checks that iterations meet a minimum.\n\n**Line 121**: Token expiry timestamp\n```java\nthis.tokenExpiryTimestamp = tokenCallback.tokenExpiryTimestamp();\n```\nAnd at line 79:\n```java\nprivate Long tokenExpiryTimestamp;\n```\nThe token expiry timestamp is stored but I don't see where it's validated against the current time. Looking more carefully - the tokenExpiryTimestamp is used in `SaslServerAuthenticator.java` at line 685-696. Let me look at that...\n\nIn SaslServerAuthenticator, the credential expiration is used:\n```java\nLong credentialExpirationMs = (Long) saslServer\n        .getNegotiatedProperty(SaslInternalConfigs.CREDENTIAL_LIFETIME_MS_SASL_NEGOTIATED_PROPERTY_KEY);\n```\n\nBut this is just for session lifetime calculation, not for actual expiry validation. The issue is that the token expiry timestamp is retrieved but never validated. An attacker could use an expired token and the SCRAM server would still accept it.\n\n**DEFECT 1: Missing token expiry validation in ScramSaslServer**\n\nThe code retrieves the token expiry timestamp and passes it along for session lifetime tracking, but never actually checks whether the token has already expired before accepting the authentication.\n\n## 2. SaslServerAuthenticator.java\n\nLooking at the re\n\n-authentication logic and principal checking, I see at lines 315-316 there's a flag that marks whether token authentication was used:\n\n```java\nif (ScramMechanism.isScram(saslMechanism) && Boolean.parseBoolean((String) saslServer.getNegotiatedProperty(ScramLoginModule.TOKEN_AUTH_CONFIG))) {\n    principal.tokenAuthenticated(true);\n}\n```\n\nHowever, this also lacks any expiry validation. Further down at lines 465-466, there's a check ensuring the principal identity remains consistent across re-authentication attempts, which is a good safeguard. The principal construction itself pulls together the authenticated identity, but without timestamp validation, expired credentials still pass through.\n\nIn CredentialCache.java, the caching mechanism stores credentials without any expiration logic. The `put` and `get` methods have no TTL or validity checks, meaning revoked or expired credentials would persist in the cache indefinitely and continue being used for subsequent authentications.\n\nLooking at AclAuthorizer.scala next, I'm examining the authorization logic to identify potential security gaps.\n\nThe `denyAll` method checks if there's a wildcard deny rule present, which seems straightforward. However, the `allowLiteral` method has a concerning pattern - it returns true immediately for wildcard resources without checking deny rules, then for specific resources it verifies they're not in the deny list and don't have dominant prefixed denies. This asymmetry could be exploited if wildcard handling isn't consistent across the authorization flow.\n\nThe `allowAny` method iterates through allow rules and calls `allowLiteral` for each one, which means a wildcard in the allow set would bypass deny checks entirely. Tracing through the full authorization logic in `authorizeAction` would clarify whether this is intentional or a vulnerability. the issue: when a wildcard resource matches in the allow rules, it immediately returns true without evaluating any deny ACLs. This means a user with a wildcard allow can bypass specific deny rules on that resource. The logic only checks deny rules for non-wildcard resources, creating a security gap where wildcard allows take precedence over all denies. Looking at the authorization flow in `authorizeAction`, the check is whether the principal is a super user or if ACLs allow access. The `aclsAllowAccess` logic evaluates three conditions: either no ACLs exist and the config permits it, or there's no deny ACL paired with at least one allow ACL. The `denyAclExists` and `allowAclExists` methods determine whether matching deny or allow ACLs are present for the resource.\n\nI notice `allowAny` isn't invoked in `authorizeAction` itself—it's only used in `authorizeByResourceType`, which is a separate authorization path. For the current method, the focus is on whether `isEmptyAclAndAuthorized` returns true when no ACLs are found, which depends on the configuration settings.\n\nThe logic then checks if a deny ACL exists for the operation, and separately verifies if an allow ACL exists. For certain operations like DESCRIBE, it expands the check to include related operations (READ, WRITE, DELETE, ALTER) that would also grant access.\n\nThe authorization decision combines these checks: access is granted if the principal is a superuser, or if no deny ACL exists and at least one matching allow ACL is found. {"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-haiku-4-5-20251001","id":"msg_01Hnpo9sqjFpeZ2fjB8zALix","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"thinking","thinking":"I've created the review report with 4 identified defects:\n\n1. **ScramSaslServer.java:121** - Missing token expiry validation (critical)\n2. **CredentialCache.java:25** - Type confusion in createCache (high)\n3. **SaslServerAuthenticator.java:315** - Missing token expiry validation during principal construction (high)\n4. **AclAuthorizer.scala:572** - Missing wildcard host matching validation (high)\n\nWait, I'm not entirely confident about the 4th one. Let me reconsider it...\n\nActually, looking at the code again:\n```scala\n(acl.host == host || acl.host == AclEntry.WILDCARD_HOST)\n```\n\nThis checks if the ACL's host matches the request host OR if the ACL has a wildcard host. This is correct - if the ACL says \"allow from any host (*)\", then any host should match.\n\nAdding `|| host == AclEntry.WILDCARD_HOST` would check if the request is from a wildcard host, which doesn't make sense. The host in the request would be the client's actual host, not a wildcard.\n\nSo I think that 4th defect is not accurate. Let me remove it and reconsider if there are other issues...\n\nActually, let me think about this more carefully. In the authorization logic, is there any place where a wildcard is bypassed?\n\nLooking at the `authorizeByResourceType` method, the logic seems correct. It checks for deny rules first, then allow rules.\n\nThe `allowAny` method checks if there's any allow ACL that's not denied by a more specific deny.\n\nHmm, actually, thinking about the `allowLiteral` method again:\n```scala\nprivate def allowLiteral(literalName: String, denyLiterals: ArrayBuffer[immutable.Set[String]],\n                        denyPrefixes: ArrayBuffer[immutable.Set[String]]): Boolean = {\n  literalName match {\n    case ResourcePattern.WILDCARD_RESOURCE => true\n    case _ => !denyLiterals.exists(_.contains(literalName)) && !hasDominantPrefixedDeny(literalName, denyPrefixes)\n  }\n}\n```\n\nThis returns true for wildcard resources without checking denyLiterals or denyPrefixes. The method bypasses deny checks when the resource is a wildcard, which could be a security issue. However, this is only called from `allowAny` which is used in `authorizeByResourceType`, not in `authorizeAction`. The denyAll check at the beginning of `authorizeByResourceType` does check if the wildcard is denied, so the wildcard bypass might be mitigated there.\n\nBut looking deeper, there's a potential vulnerability: if there's an ALLOW for \"*\" and a DENY for a specific prefix, the wildcard allow would bypass the prefix deny check entirely.\n\nLet me trace through the actual logic flow. The code first checks if the wildcard resource is denied at lines 372-375, returning DENIED immediately if so. Then it separately evaluates prefix denies at lines 382-384. If both the non-wildcard denies and prefix denies are empty, it proceeds to check allows. Otherwise, it continues to evaluate whether the specific resource matches any deny rules before checking allows. The issue is in how the wildcard allow is handled—it appears to skip the deny prefix validation that should apply to specific resources. wildcards should also consider prefix denies. The `allowLiteral` function checks if a specific resource name is allowed given the deny rules, but for wildcards it's only verifying the wildcard itself isn't denied—it's not checking whether prefix denies would block access to resources matching that wildcard.\n\nThe real issue is whether prefix denies should override wildcard allows. If I have `ALLOW \"*\"` but `DENY prefix \"foo\"`, then accessing a resource like \"foo-bar\" should be denied. However, `authorizeByResourceType` doesn't have a specific resource name to evaluate against, so it's unclear what the correct authorization should be when the answer depends on which specific resource is being accessed. my initial concern about the 4th defect. Looking more carefully at the authorization logic, `authorizeByResourceType` determines whether an operation is generally permitted for a resource type, while `authorizeAction` handles specific resource checks. The method correctly returns true when there's a wildcard allow, even if specific resources are denied—this is the intended behavior since it's checking type-level authorization, not instance-level.
